  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_27122b58-e84b-4774-9bff-ee8e25ba84ad'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9023128293040021348">Based on the troubadour culture that rose in Provence during the High Middle Ages, this panoramic, absorbing novel beautifully creates an alternate version of the medieval world.<br/><br/>The matriarchal, cultured land of Arbonne is rent by a feud between its two most powerful dukes, the noble troubador Bertran de Talair and Urte de Miraval, over long-dead Aelis, lover of one, wife </span>
  <span id="freeText9023128293040021348" style="display:none">Based on the troubadour culture that rose in Provence during the High Middle Ages, this panoramic, absorbing novel beautifully creates an alternate version of the medieval world.<br /><br />The matriarchal, cultured land of Arbonne is rent by a feud between its two most powerful dukes, the noble troubador Bertran de Talair and Urte de Miraval, over long-dead Aelis, lover of one, wife of the other and once heir to the country's throne.<br /><br />To the north lies militaristic Gorhaut, whose inhabitants worship the militant god Corannos and are ruled by corrupt, womanizing King Ademar. His chief advisor, the high priest of Corannos, is determined to eradicate the worship of a female deity, whose followers live to the south.<br /><br />Into this cauldron of brewing disaster comes the mysterious Gorhaut mercenary Blaise, who takes service with Bertran and averts an attempt on his life. The revelation of Blaise's lineage and a claim for sanctuary by his sister-in-law sets the stage for a brutal clash between the two cultures. Intertwined is the tale of a young woman troubadour whose role suggests the sweep of the drama to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9023128293040021348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

He is the only man in the story with whose child she would have AND not tell. Bertan would have been faithful to Rosala, so not Bertran. Who else is of consequence?<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/42803606-gillian-robinson"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/42803606-gillian-robinson">Gillian Robinson</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I'm about 90% certain it's Ariane. There's three candidates at the end--Rinette, the strong political alliance; Lucianna, Blaise's old flame; and Aria<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I'm about 90% certain it's Ariane. There's three candidates at the end--Rinette, the strong political alliance; Lucianna, Blaise's old flame; and Ariane, the woman he's in love with--and Lisseut's vidan mentions that she was close to both his wives. Lucianna doesn't seem the type to get buddy-buddy with Lisseut (and, in a meta sense, her story's been neatly tied off with the red rose at the tournament). Thierry could've died or stepped aside, and Rinette might have gone back to Rian's Island (maybe in the wake of Beatriz's death?). <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57670849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1857123283701870303">I am in awe. This might be the best book I've read this year. It might be one of the best books I've ever read.<br/><br/>If you like epics, this is for you. Romance, intrigue, artistic expression, mystery, combat scenes, sex, violence, passion, compassion, bitter revenge, redemption. It's all here.<br/><br/>This book was what makes fantasy great. It is what makes historical fiction great. A perfect blend of the two, with very human elements there to give life to the characters. The reader is constantly on the edge </span>
  <span id="freeText1857123283701870303" style="display:none">I am in awe. This might be the best book I've read this year. It might be one of the best books I've ever read.<br /><br />If you like epics, this is for you. Romance, intrigue, artistic expression, mystery, combat scenes, sex, violence, passion, compassion, bitter revenge, redemption. It's all here.<br /><br />This book was what makes fantasy great. It is what makes historical fiction great. A perfect blend of the two, with very human elements there to give life to the characters. The reader is constantly on the edge of his/her seat, and the payoff is in spades.<br /><br />I loved <i>Tigana</i>, my first experience with Kay, when I read it last year. This tops it.<br /><br />I don't know what else to say without spoiling anything. I will say there was only one thing I didn't like about this book: that the experience had to end.......</span>
  <a data-text-id="1857123283701870303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_57670849'><a id="like_count_review_57670849" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/57670849?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">60 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F104085.A_Song_for_Arbonne">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1600039739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1600039739" />
    <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries"><img alt="Mayim de Vries" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560508357p2/50424456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1600039739?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayim de Vries" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries">Mayim de Vries</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451453327" href="/book/show/876692.A_Song_for_Arbonne">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1600039739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15606218152578856973">Artisans, it seems, are very important for Kay. Tigana had musicians, Sarantine duology a mosaicist as the main protagonist, in <b>Arbonne, fashioned after the medieval Provence and Occitania, troubadours are in the middle of things</b> (along with joglars and trobairitz, that is female performers). <b>With them, the ideals of court love, gallantry, poetry, tournaments, knights and dames and all the associated drama constitutes a backbone of this novel.</b> <br/><br/>To give you a proper context: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5031312.Dante_Alighieri' title='Dante Alighieri' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dante Alighieri</a> saw th</span>
  <span id="freeText15606218152578856973" style="display:none">Artisans, it seems, are very important for Kay. Tigana had musicians, Sarantine duology a mosaicist as the main protagonist, in <b>Arbonne, fashioned after the medieval Provence and Occitania, troubadours are in the middle of things</b> (along with joglars and trobairitz, that is female performers). <b>With them, the ideals of court love, gallantry, poetry, tournaments, knights and dames and all the associated drama constitutes a backbone of this novel.</b> <br /><br />To give you a proper context: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5031312.Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri" rel="nofollow noopener">Dante Alighieri</a> saw the love of his life, a woman named Beatrice, 4 times altogether (this is what you'll find in his biographies, however hard it is to ascertain the truth). Once, when they were kids, and then three times more (including her wedding to Simon dei Bardi and one encounter on a street when she did not respond to his greeting). Beatrice wasn't Dante’s lover, not even his friend or merely an acquaintance and yet, against those odds she was the ultimate love of his life (as you know if you have read his poetry). At the same time, Dante was married, had children and numerous lovers. But his love, and devotion and art — all that was only for Beatrice (even after her death). <br /><br />Another celebrated poet, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16944348.Petrarca" title="Petrarca" rel="nofollow noopener">Petrarca</a>, was 3 years junior to his beloved Laura (he was 23 and she was 26 when they met in 1327 in Avignon). She was married and had 12 children, but Petrarca loved her ardently his whole life. She was the axis around which his art revolved (his poetry is divided into <i>In vita di madonna Laura</i> and <i>In morte di madonna Laura</i>). <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1681691.Jaufr__Rudel" title="Jaufré Rudel" rel="nofollow noopener">Jaufré Rudel</a>, one of the most famous medieval troubadours, didn't even see the love of his life in person - he has been shown a small picture of a lady from Tripoli. This was enough to infuse him with love and all knightly intentions. He joined a crusade but arrived in Tripoli already dying. When the lady in question learned about this, she came to his deathbed, offered him a ring and he died gazing at her. Soon after she became a nun. <br /><br />All the above stories are true, and if you find them ridiculous, you will have problems reading this novel. In the Song for Arbonne Kay wishes to emulate this ambiance and state of mind, creating a country characterised by a soft, somewhat effeminate culture, ruled by a woman, worshipping a goddess and torn apart by a decades-long feud between two of the most powerful lords over another woman. <b>You need to be open to the romantic notions and rules of medieval courtly love to accept the premises for this design.</b> <br /><br />This <b>female-dominated Arbonne is the juxtaposed with Gorhaut espousing all things male</b>, including masculine, military-driven culture and a warrior deity. Here you have <b>the other element of the book design, a religious war somewhat mimicking the events of the Albigensian crusade</b>. Conflict is imminent between the two states and the focal point of the brewing war is a young Gorhautian mercenary resisting in Arbonne, one Blaise.<br /><br />I fond Blaise a likable hero, conflicted and marred by his past, but likable (also at times hilarious). It soon transpires that he is much more than an unassuming sword for hire and as the plot thickens and connections become apparent, Blaise is forced to pave his own way to save both countries from peril. <br /><br />Sadly for me, <b>Song of Arbonne, consists mainly of half measures and does not meet the standards I expected from Kay. He merely scratches the surface of so many different issues that should have been properly explored or uses shortcuts regarding plot-twists and character developments</b> that after finishing the last chapter I was in a truly foul mood. <b>Beautiful language and interesting scene here or there are saving graces, but overall the book is of average quality.</b> <br /><br />Feminine Arbonne and masculine Gorhaut were perhaps meant to show that taking things to the extreme is harmful in both ways, that male and female element must be balanced, but you will not find it in the book. The clash comes and goes without any reflection of what had caused it (and the religious element doesn't really fit or reverberate properly). <br /><br />This is to some extent caused by the <b>major weakness of the novel, i.e. the villains. Villains here are pathetic evil creatures.</b> We have two of them, the king of Gorhaut is a monster of rare quality, while the High Elder, the religious leader, is just evil period. You will not find back stories, nuances or details that would invite the reader to understand (if not endure) their rationale. There is just everything you can imagine inserted into this character to ensure that the readers hate him without compunction. And Kay I know is able to write complex black characters to fall in love with! <br /><br />Finally, <b>I had some qualms with the main love story of the book lying at the foundation of the unfolding drama</b>. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[If you think about it, we read on thinking that the main engine for events is a drama between two men who had the same woman - we are led on believing that only one loved her. We even pity the poor girl and her fate. But this is proven to be false! Firstly, Aelis what a horrible, spoiled brat that was! She ruined lives of two men, her own parents, and her cousin, and endangered the very existence of her country on a whim - not to mention the fact that she deprived her daughter of a father! two fathers in fact! grandparents... a proper family... everything, because in her sick head there was no place for anything save revenge and lack of forgiveness. <br /><br />Her second victim was her lover, Bertrand, who carried the torch of her love for twenty-some years, even though the feeling continues against himself. This is the reason why he beds all the women he can; to find if not a replacement, then somebody who can make him forget, if only for one night. Undoubtedly Bertand is also a victim of this tragic love, just like his dead paramour.<br /><br />He is also a victim because Aelis locked him within a world of hatred for her husband Urte. Urte and Bertand become like king and yang, unable to live lives independently of each other: “they needed each other to keep alive the clear, bitter memories of that long-ago year, to give each other, however inexplicable it might appear, a reason to continue living.”<br /><br />But Urte, Urte is the greatest victim. Only in the last chapter is it revealed that the portrait of a crude, unfeeling husband Aelis painted has been false. Urte loved all these years, but because he himself was more Gorhautian in demeanour and habits than Arbonnais nobody realised this was the case. He loved but was not loved in return. And Aelis attributed him only the hatred she felt.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> And what? And nothing. The whole thing is shrugged over. Nothing is explored. As if the package of love is more important than the very thing. This is also a shortcut not worthy of the author I know and admire. <br /><br />In the end, I found the<b> book simplistic. Several shortcuts and yet the finale is not neat; too many flapping loose ends.</b> I am not sure if Kay himself knew where the story would go because we didn't have his usual foreshadowing indicating mastery of every detail in the design. <br /><br /><b>Promising beginning, and pathetic ending. Some fun in between. No swoon though, so don't start your journey with Kay here.</b> You might wish to try <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1752216302?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Tigana</a> first or the Sarantine Mosaic duology set in Byzantium-like world: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2112183584?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Sailing to Sarantium</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2130408942?type=review#rating_133793954" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of Emperors</a>.<br /><br />----<br /><br />This was a buddy read with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/18605646-a-song-for-arbonne-october-2017" rel="nofollow noopener">Kay Squad</a> at FBR.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15606218152578856973" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2066574503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2066574503" />
    <a title="Choko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27962805-choko"><img alt="Choko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475642755p2/27962805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2066574503?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Choko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Choko" href="/user/show/27962805-choko">Choko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11466357-a-song-for-arbonne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27962805-choko?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27962805-choko?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27962805-choko?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2066574503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2219016893308061861"><b>*** 4.75 ***<br/><br/>A Buddy Read with the Fantasy Buddy Reads Group, because we love G. G. Kay!</b><br/><br/>Have you ever read a book and felt completely inadequate to write a review for it? Not that the book was that overwhelming in scope or plot lines, nor because it tackled some deep philosophical issues whose gravity would be impossible to put in several paragraphs. The ineptitude I find myself in, is because this is the forth book written by Kay I have read in the last couple of months and with each volume I be</span>
  <span id="freeText2219016893308061861" style="display:none"><b>*** 4.75 ***<br /><br />A Buddy Read with the Fantasy Buddy Reads Group, because we love G. G. Kay!</b><br /><br />Have you ever read a book and felt completely inadequate to write a review for it? Not that the book was that overwhelming in scope or plot lines, nor because it tackled some deep philosophical issues whose gravity would be impossible to put in several paragraphs. The ineptitude I find myself in, is because this is the forth book written by Kay I have read in the last couple of months and with each volume I become more and more enamored with his way of balancing Historical Fiction, some Fantasy elements, Romance, and Artistic expression in a hybrid which could not be put into a box, but feels like each of its parent's genres most favorite child. Even his unique outlook on Historical Fantasy is not enough to explain the magic he has woven in his tales. It is the way he tells the story, with an obvious nod to the old Bards, but with an updated language, using a prose which is open for all levels of readers - from novices to sages, using finesse and complexity more in the plot-lines than in the way he conveys them, crafting characters whose personalities vary on the gray scale of attributes, none of them perfect, none of them completely villainous, and makes us care for them even when we don't particularly want to. His ability to use simple words and moments, in a sea of overwhelming action and intrigue, which are so devastatingly poignant and touching, that make you want to weep with their raw exposure of the humanity in everyone, big or small, significant or just one of the crowd... His biggest talent lays with the knowledge of how to employee the writing style of the old masters and amplify the effect with his ability to pull it back and punctuate it by stripping the prose down to bare bones in the most consequential of junctures. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"..."“He was still on his feet, and before him was a man who stood in the path of...what? Of a great many things, his own dream of Gorhaut not least of all. Of what his home should be, in the eyes of the world, in the sight of Corannos, in his own soul. He had said this two nights ago, words very like this, King Daufridi of Valensa. He's been asked if he loved his country.<br />He did. He loved it with a heart that ached like an old man's fingers in rain, hurting for the Gorhaut of his own vision, a land worthy of the god who had chosen it, and of the honor of men. Not a place of scheming wiles, of a degraded, sensuously corrupt king, of people dispossessed of their lands by a cowardly treaty, or of ugly designs under the false, perverted aegis of Corannos for nothing less than annihilation here south of the mountains.”..."</i>
</b><br /><br />As with the previous books, this one takes place in a Fantasy world, whose England, Normandy and Provence are reflected in Valensa, Gorhaut and Arbonne around the 11-13th centuries. It is inspired by the culture of Provence at the time of the Troubadours and the age of gallantry, leading to the first cultural introduction of love as that of a young man pining for a married woman of status, as the ideal for true love, even if it is unrequited... It also speaks of the very heavily Pagan and matriarchal culture of the land, despite the introduction of the more male-oriented and homogeneous. Once again, by invoking the history of the Albigensian Crusade in spirit, and the influence of the never ending wars between the Anglo-Saxons and Normans, as well as the later political maneuvers in order to bring the South to France, we are given a microcosm of those events served with the nuances of the eternal battle between the Feminine and Masculine, be it in human culture or in celestial divinity. G. G. Kay always gives us a juxtaposition between the Male and Female, but he never gives us his answers as to which side he is on, or does he push us into choosing. In my opinion, he makes a beautiful argument for their complimentary strengths and weaknesses, advocating in his unobtrusive way for the need of their balance.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"..."“We must be what we are, or we become our enemies. ” ..."</i>
</b><br /><br />Equal to his incredible ability of finding beauty even in the ugliest of situations, Kay views the world with a dose of irony and cynicism, which attributes the Male and Female sides with as many devious and uncompromising choices for achieving their goals as their counterparts. They do not shy from using every weapon in their arsenal, be it a weapon, poison, sex, flirtation, deception or political manipulation, disfigurement or murder, each with equal ruthlessness, that we see they only differ in the manner they are performed and the ultimate ideal they strive for, but not in the paths of obtaining them... Women and men are equally beautiful, desirable, smart, talented and strong, the author being able to show us positive qualities even in the most despicable of characters, that every loss, no matter on which side, becomes a wound in the reader's heart... If I only had one wish, it would be for more authors to be as thoughtful and purposeful in the way they structure a story, so I may never ever again read a book which might let me down:):):) <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"..."“For all his frustrations and his chronic sense of being overburdened. He was proud of that; he’d always felt that it was worth doing a task properly if it was worth doing at all. That was part of his problem, of course; that was why he ended up with so much to do. It was also the source of his own particular pride: he knew--and he was certain they knew that there was no one else who could handle details such as these as well as he.” ..."</i>
</b><br /><br />Now I wish you all Happy Reading and may you always find what you Need in the pages of a Good Book!!! </span>
  <a data-text-id="2219016893308061861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3827360872196891421">I am so, so incredibly torn about this one. It's a Guy Gavriel Kay historical fantasy novel, so of course it's a beautiful masterpiece. But it's a Guy Gavriel Kay historical fantasy novel, so of course it will inevitably be compared to <i>Tigana</i>. It simply does not provide the sublimely perfect satisfaction of that other epic, and the ending here seemed actually rather uninspired. Neither does it quite measure up to the Sarantium duology in my mind.<br/><br/>It's obviously a fantastic work, but I need time t</span>
  <span id="freeText3827360872196891421" style="display:none">I am so, so incredibly torn about this one. It's a Guy Gavriel Kay historical fantasy novel, so of course it's a beautiful masterpiece. But it's a Guy Gavriel Kay historical fantasy novel, so of course it will inevitably be compared to <i>Tigana</i>. It simply does not provide the sublimely perfect satisfaction of that other epic, and the ending here seemed actually rather uninspired. Neither does it quite measure up to the Sarantium duology in my mind.<br /><br />It's obviously a fantastic work, but I need time to think to settle on just how fantastic.</span>

<div id="review_730935424" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/730935424" />
    <a title="Em Lost In Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11166121-em-lost-in-books"><img alt="Em Lost In Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522743986p2/11166121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/730935424?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Em Lost In Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Em Lost In Books" href="/user/show/11166121-em-lost-in-books">Em Lost In Books</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007342051" href="/book/show/8013371-a-song-for-arbonne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=stand-alone-read">stand-alone-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=1990-99">1990-99</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer730935424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9093902712269564233">My fourth book by GGK and once again I liked the the world, story and the characters created by him. This is a beautiful story of love, loyalty, honor, family, courage, sacrifice, betrayal and survival. <br/><br/>First I must confess I was not impressed in first five chapters but after that I was unable to put it down. It took me a little time to understand the plot. Arbonne a land famous for its troubadours which is ruled by a woman and worship a goddess. Their passion for their country is remarkable. It</span>
  <span id="freeText9093902712269564233" style="display:none">My fourth book by GGK and once again I liked the the world, story and the characters created by him. This is a beautiful story of love, loyalty, honor, family, courage, sacrifice, betrayal and survival. <br /><br />First I must confess I was not impressed in first five chapters but after that I was unable to put it down. It took me a little time to understand the plot. Arbonne a land famous for its troubadours which is ruled by a woman and worship a goddess. Their passion for their country is remarkable. It is a total contrast to the land of Gorhaut which has a patriarchal society. the high priest of Gorhaut, who hates women and despised the matriarchy in Arbonne, wants to destroy Arbonne and rule it. It is his hatred that bring these two nations on the cusp of the war and whole story revolve around this impending war.<br /><br />Kay is famous for using historical places. He used southeastern France for this one. The world that he has portrayed in this book is beautiful, unforgettable and very resonant. Characters are very good and their narrative describe the different events of past for us. Kay’s writing is very gorgeous, the way he describe people’s emotions is simply breathtaking. His writing is a reader’s delight. And again I couldn’t stop myself marveling upon how much strength he gives to his female characters. <br /><br />This was an enjoyable read for me but still it didn’t touched me like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104089.Tigana" title="Tigana by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Tigana</a>, didn’t made me cry like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104101.The_Lions_of_Al_Rassan" title="The Lions of Al-Rassan by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lions of Al-Rassan</a> or kept me on the edge the whole time due its political drama like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7139892.Under_Heaven" title="Under Heaven by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Under Heaven</a>. A man whose hatred inspired him to end a country because they worship a Goddess and respect women did not settled well with me. <br /><br />My most favorite character in this book was Rosala. She was an extremely intelligent and clever woman. Her courage when she faced the most feared man in country was truly awesome. &amp; then taking the most difficult decision of her life, putting herself and a country in a dangerous situation was very brave decision.<br /><br />Not GGK's best work but still an admirable read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9093902712269564233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517593127" href="/book/show/533820.A_Song_For_Arbonne">review of another edition</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=character-driven">character-driven</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=heartbreaking">heartbreaking</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2186299772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer756805329841864786"><blockquote>Two countries, two deities. <br/><br/>Arbonne: Southerly, warm, goddess-worshipping, and filled with songs of love. <br/><br/>Gorhaut: Northern, harsh, god-worshipping, and ruled by a cruel and twisted king. <br/><br/>They couldn’t seem more different. <br/><br/>Blaise de Garsenc has a foot in both lands: A younger-son noble of Gorhaut, he has come to Arbonne to work as a mercenary coran. As the two countries come closer to war with one another, he begins to recognize a disturbing parallel between them. Each has internal rivalries tha</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText756805329841864786" style="display:none"><blockquote>Two countries, two deities. <br /><br />Arbonne: Southerly, warm, goddess-worshipping, and filled with songs of love. <br /><br />Gorhaut: Northern, harsh, god-worshipping, and ruled by a cruel and twisted king. <br /><br />They couldn’t seem more different. <br /><br />Blaise de Garsenc has a foot in both lands: A younger-son noble of Gorhaut, he has come to Arbonne to work as a mercenary coran. As the two countries come closer to war with one another, he begins to recognize a disturbing parallel between them. Each has internal rivalries that threaten their unity when they need it most. </blockquote>How do you sum up a Guy Gavriel Kay novel? <br /><br />This one features his usual mix of many POV’s (all clearly distinguished), many twists and complications, rich and detailed worldbuilding and mythology, beautiful writing, and thoughtful and insightful storytelling. <br /><br />This was a combination of tough and comforting for me, as it has a heavy focus on loss and grief, and I am currently mourning for a friend who died six weeks ago. (This is partly why the few reviews I've managed to write lately are so short and unspirited: my heart just isn't in them right now.) I'd put the balance of this story more on "comforting" at this time; I'm glad this book called to me now. <br /><br />So far GGK is three for three with me. I’m looking forward to when I can squeeze another of his books into my schedule! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="756805329841864786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_57413007" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57413007" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/188224-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187751946p2/188224.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57413007?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/188224-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57413007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7987911959086193857">This book is like a nice painting splattered with mud. It's a great story - intrigue, war, love, mystery, politics, tension, regret, impending doom, death, surprises, clear good guys, clear bad guys, and some you aren't sure about. And the setting is nice if you like traditional fantasy of the medieval/Renaissance style - more swords and nobles than magic and monsters. I enjoyed it. But the author threw up a lot of unnecessary chaff between me and my enjoyment of the story. It wasn't enough to r</span>
  <span id="freeText7987911959086193857" style="display:none">This book is like a nice painting splattered with mud. It's a great story - intrigue, war, love, mystery, politics, tension, regret, impending doom, death, surprises, clear good guys, clear bad guys, and some you aren't sure about. And the setting is nice if you like traditional fantasy of the medieval/Renaissance style - more swords and nobles than magic and monsters. I enjoyed it. But the author threw up a lot of unnecessary chaff between me and my enjoyment of the story. It wasn't enough to ruin it, but was enough to detract and annoy.<br /><br />The most notable interference comes from regular injections of overly florid language. He tries so hard to say something grand or majestic sometimes and it just comes out so cheezy and overdone that it makes you groan. Luckily most of the writing is just fine, good even. But every twenty pages or so a real turkey of a sentence comes out of nowhere to smack you like a log of bologna. To wit: "Lisseut of Vezet realized, with a cold dawning of despair, that her heart was gone from her. It had left without her knowing, like a bird in winter, flying north to a hopelessly wrong destination where no haven or warmth or welcome could even be imagined." Booooooo! Here's another: "Signe reached for sleep that night as a woman reaching for the last gentle lover of her days." Whaaat? Nooooo! I'm glad he didn't describe her brushing her teeth. "She polished each tooth like the cherished memories of a stolen child." <br /><br />Another distraction is the unnecessary use of the present tense to narrate action happening amongst the bad guy northerners while using the regular past tense everywhere else. So you get "Galbert scowls and walks out of the room" instead of "Galbert scowled and walked out of the room." It's strange to read the present tense in a story to start with, and the abrupt transition between tenses makes you stumble, un-immersing you temporarily each time.<br /><br />One thing that bothered me that might not bother others is the songs. I don't know what I was thinking - the word "song" is right there in the title. I guess I thought it was a metaphor or something. Nope, it's songs. I've disliked songs in books ever since The Hobbit:"Tom Bom, jolly Tom, Tom Bombadillo!" Grrr, I choke you! Luckily there aren't more than a few songs in this book, but they're real stinkers, and we're supposed to get all wistful about them. One of them mentions "choice meats". Choice meats? Seriously?! Awful!<br /><br />Anyway, hooray for battles, family rivalries, court intrigue, etc. Boo for peacocky flourishes and meat songs.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7987911959086193857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_583072776" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583072776" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/583072776?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11466357-a-song-for-arbonne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583072776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9143632856757319320">I can understand people who don't like Guy Gavriel Kay's work. I think I've said it before, but there are definite quirks of style, ways he plots and deals with characters, that can drive even me mad in the wrong mood -- which is why I first picked this up to reread in April, and now it's November when I've finally finished. I do love most of Kay's work when I'm in the right mood, though, and <i>A Song for Arbonne</i> is additionally up my street because of the Court of Love, the troubadours, all the s</span>
  <span id="freeText9143632856757319320" style="display:none">I can understand people who don't like Guy Gavriel Kay's work. I think I've said it before, but there are definite quirks of style, ways he plots and deals with characters, that can drive even me mad in the wrong mood -- which is why I first picked this up to reread in April, and now it's November when I've finally finished. I do love most of Kay's work when I'm in the right mood, though, and <i>A Song for Arbonne</i> is additionally up my street because of the Court of Love, the troubadours, all the stuff that's part of the Arthurian legends as well once they hit France.<br /><br />I don't think, though, that I fell for this book quite as much as I have for some of the others. I'm not as attached to Bertran as to Alessan or Diarmuid; not held in sympathy with his rival and enemy, Urté de Miraval, as I am with Brandin in <i>Tigana</i>, not until the very end of the book; not really caught up in Blaise's story, in his fight for a throne, as I am with Aileron's or Alessan's. There's some good stuff here, but some promising background characters didn't really come to full bloom for me -- Valery, Rudel, even Hirnan -- and despite the women-centric society, we didn't have female characters as striking as Catriana or as pivotal as Kim. Rosala was probably the female character I was most interested in, but she comes somewhat late into her own, and I felt as though I should be more aware of the other female characters. They shadowed the story, they were behind it, and yet they weren't the visible drivers. Not quite the story Kay was aiming to tell, I think.<br /><br />Still, all of that sounds harsh, when I really do enjoy this book. When Kay gives you a scene, a character, a moment, he expects you to remember. He <i>will</i> use it. One character's chance word reveals another's secret, one introspective passage becomes suddenly important. It's a rich world he creates, and some parts of it dance with life -- and ache with sadness.<br /><br />It's just, it does pale when held up against some of his other books. Even the flaws of <i>The Summer Tree</i> and the other two books of that trilogy are brilliant. I was a little surprised to like this book possibly less this time than last, which may be some combination of mood and timing; normally I like Kay's work better with each reread.<br /><br />Regardless, there's always something to treasure in Kay's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9143632856757319320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_78722457" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78722457" />
    <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper"><img alt="Kat  Hooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533675060p2/2177891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78722457?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat  Hooper" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78722457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18056631507527535129">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>.<br/><br/>Blaise, a sellsword from Gorhaut (a violent and chauvinistic northern country), has moved to the warmer country of Arbonne. Blaise doesn’t have much appreciation or tolerance for Arbonne’s womanly culture which is highly influenced by the Court of Love. He also doesn’t have much hope that Arbonne – which values singers over soldiers, and troubadours over troops – will put up much of a fight if Gorhaut decides to try to eradicate Arbonne’s goddess worship. </span>
  <span id="freeText18056631507527535129" style="display:none">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />Blaise, a sellsword from Gorhaut (a violent and chauvinistic northern country), has moved to the warmer country of Arbonne. Blaise doesn’t have much appreciation or tolerance for Arbonne’s womanly culture which is highly influenced by the Court of Love. He also doesn’t have much hope that Arbonne – which values singers over soldiers, and troubadours over troops – will put up much of a fight if Gorhaut decides to try to eradicate Arbonne’s goddess worship. But what is Blaise doing in Arbonne anyway? Is he hiding, or is he spying?<br /><br />I immediately fell in love with Blaise who we meet as he’s commanding a small group of soldiers who are sneaking onto Arbonne’s goddess’s holy island. They plan to kidnap a troubadour who is sulking after being humiliated by his employer’s wife who screamed loudly when he tried to make good on her disingenuous flirtations. Blaise thinks all of this is incredibly ridiculous and he has little confidence in the men he commands. How can they be manly when they live in this gentle culture?<br /><br />I guess I liked Blaise because I was thinking the same thing – these guys are a bunch of wusses and this courtly society is shallow and immoral. But Blaise and I learned that when their lives and lifestyle are threatened, the passionate people of Arbonne respond.<br /><br />Much of the novel’s background information is delivered by several characters’ emotional interior monologues, a technique I like when it’s not overdone to the point that it really slows the action. It was nearly over-the-top in A Song for Arbonne, but I liked Blaise so much that it worked for me when we were in his head. I can imagine, however, that this style won’t suit all readers. Kay invests his work with a lot of passion, and sometimes I can sense the manipulation of my emotions with his fervid prose. A writer is supposed to elicit feeling from me, but I don’t want to notice it happening.<br /><br />The plot of A Song for Arbonne was original and interesting though some of the antagonists’ motivations, revealed at the end, seemed contrived. I might have believed them if they’d been hinted at earlier, and this would also have helped the bad guys not seem so one-dimensionally bad. <br /><br />But overall, A Song for Arbonne is a beautiful, sumptuous, emotional novel. I listened to this on audio, performed by Euan Morton. This was the first time I’d heard Mr. Morton and I thought he was perfect for this title. He did a terrific job.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18056631507527535129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_683403884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/683403884" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/683403884?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer683403884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1723123770219022345"><b>A reimagining of the Crusades against music-loving, female-dominated Provence</b><br/>This is another of Guy Gavriel Kay's historical reimaginings of dramatic and tumultuous historical periods in European history, in this case the Albigensian Crusade of Pope Innocent III against the Cathar heretics of Languedoc (in southern France), with the troubadours and Court of Love and elevated status of women, all anathema to the Catholic Church of the times. GKK goes to great lengths to depict this world in lush </span>
  <span id="freeText1723123770219022345" style="display:none"><b>A reimagining of the Crusades against music-loving, female-dominated Provence</b><br />This is another of Guy Gavriel Kay's historical reimaginings of dramatic and tumultuous historical periods in European history, in this case the Albigensian Crusade of Pope Innocent III against the Cathar heretics of Languedoc (in southern France), with the troubadours and Court of Love and elevated status of women, all anathema to the Catholic Church of the times. GKK goes to great lengths to depict this world in lush detail and prose, much like his other historical fantasies Tigana (Renaissance Italy), the Lions of Al-Rassan (Moorish Spain), and Sarantium Mosaic (clash between Byzantine Empire and Ostrogothic Empire of Italy).<br /><br />As his many fans can attest to, his writing is rich, lyrical, emotion-laden, and colorful. This can either work for or against him, depending on reader taste. I thought it was very effective in Tigana, as the basic story was clearly understandable and the character's motivations were understandable and relatable. However, I found the machinations and motivations of the large caste of characters in A Song for Arbonne difficult to follow, especially as many have difficult-to-remember names like Bertran de Talair, Urte de Miraval, Thierry de Carenzu, Galbert de Garsanc, etc. I realize that real historical events feature just such complex and numerous casts of characters, and plotting and scheming and motivated by cultural modes of thinking and historical rights and wrongs, but it was all a bit overwhelming. <br /><br />It's always interesting to speculate on why GKK chooses to cloak and reimagine historical events with invented countries, cultures, regions, religions, and stories. Why not just write historical fiction in that case? But perhaps he can take greater artistic license by exploring the themes the run throughout European antiquity with a freer hand, though sometimes it all seems a bit unnecessary. The magical elements have become minimal since his Tolkienesque fantasy trilogy The Fionavar Tapestry, so there seems even less need for this elaborate approach to history, but his writing skills are undeniable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1723123770219022345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2753415092" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2753415092" />
    <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic"><img alt="Ivana Books Are Magic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578249636p2/56173179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2753415092?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivana Books Are Magic" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic">Ivana Books Are Magic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753415092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3407627445420004116">A Song for Arbonne was my second novel by Guy Gavrial Kay (the first one was Tigana). It is a novel I liked much better on the second reading. The first time I read it, I was in my early twenties and the book didn't resonate with me. The second time I read, I was a bit older and less moralistically inclined, so I liked it much better. There are some definite writing flaws in this one, such as cardboard villains and writing cliches but I'll get to that later. The reason why I didn't like it the f</span>
  <span id="freeText3407627445420004116" style="display:none">A Song for Arbonne was my second novel by Guy Gavrial Kay (the first one was Tigana). It is a novel I liked much better on the second reading. The first time I read it, I was in my early twenties and the book didn't resonate with me. The second time I read, I was a bit older and less moralistically inclined, so I liked it much better. There are some definite writing flaws in this one, such as cardboard villains and writing cliches but I'll get to that later. The reason why I didn't like it the first time around was because I couldn't relate to any of the characters and because the world itself felt alien to me.<br /><br />As I grew older, I learned a bit more about history of troubadour culture so on the second reading that aspect of the book was much more interesting to me. Kay is famous for taking inspiration from historical events and drawing from historical periods. Most of the time, Kay does it wonderfully. In this novel, the writer certainly created an atmosphere of a troubadour court. The clash of two cultures and countries was fascinating as well. Moreover, this time around I was better able to relate to the characters. I still think they could have been better written, but I found most of the characters more interesting and human on the reread. However, that doesn't change the fact that not that much happens in this novel. Yes, there is some political scheming and what not, but is it really exciting? No, not really. The plot twists are also solved a bit deux et machina, which is never good as is typically a sign of lazziness on the part of the writer. The world building could have been a bit better. There are a lot of references to a past event that shaped much of what happens in the novel, but the whole thing feels too nostalgic and unreal. The novel doesn't really benefit from those past references and I don't see the point to them if the writer didn't plan for another book in this universe.<br /><br />What this books lacks the most is a protagonist that you could really love and feel for. Blaise is too torn apart to offer cohesion this novel needs. He has escaped his homeland, travelled and seen things, he is supposed to be the hero and yet Blaise seems very confused and indecisive most of the time. There are a lot of female characters who basically do nothing and have no role to play. Even the one that was villain wasn't memorable at all. There are a lot of characters in this novel that could have been better developed.<br /><br /> There is something enchanting about A Song for Arbonne, the atmosphere of the book and the writing itself is, at times, quite beautiful. When you really get into it, this novel can feel like listening to a beautiful song. Nevertheless, this book does suffer from some writing flaws. It could have been better written and thought through. One gets a feeling it is a kind of book that didn't deliver on its promise. I would grate it 3.5 if the grading system of goodreads would allow it but I'll round it up to four because I have read it at least three times so far and I've grown attached to it over the years. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3407627445420004116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_777881244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/777881244" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1662632-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540855273p2/1662632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/777881244?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/1662632-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517593127" href="/book/show/533820.A_Song_For_Arbonne">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of high romance / high fantasy / historical fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=fantasy-alt-history">fantasy-alt-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer777881244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15709168392319197088"><i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is a lyrical portrayal of one tumultuous year in Arbonne, as its peace-loving people — aristocracy, mercenaries, troubadours, priests and priestesses — deal with the threat of invasion from their war-hungry neighbors to the north.<br/><br/>Much to my astonishment, some folks don’t enjoy the works of Guy Kay as much as I do. That said, the overall ratings for <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is well above four, which puts it in pretty rarified territory. Even the negative reviews of Kay usually agree </span>
  <span id="freeText15709168392319197088" style="display:none"><i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is a lyrical portrayal of one tumultuous year in Arbonne, as its peace-loving people — aristocracy, mercenaries, troubadours, priests and priestesses — deal with the threat of invasion from their war-hungry neighbors to the north.<br /><br />Much to my astonishment, some folks don’t enjoy the works of Guy Kay as much as I do. That said, the overall ratings for <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is well above four, which puts it in pretty rarified territory. Even the negative reviews of Kay usually agree that the characters are well developed, that the world is richly and lovingly detailed, and that the prose is lyrical. The negative consensus seems to be that not enough action drives the story, though.<br /><br />I can understand that. There’s a lot of talking, and a lot of introspection. The characters are trying to understand the motivations of others, so it is pretty psychological. That makes this book (and all of Kay’s that I’ve read so far) quite unusual in the universe of speculative fiction.<br /><br />There’s also a subset that dislike Kay’s portrayal of sex. It is definitely present here, although not too much in my opinion. Some of what is portrayed is somewhat kinky, too. Well, I guess to most of y’all it would be <i>very</i> kinky, but I’m a San Franciscan. But we’re talking maybe two or three scenes out of the five hundred pages. Trivial compared to George R.R. Martin’s <i> Game of Thrones</i>, for example.<br /><br />In this standalone book, Kay actually has quite a bit in common with Martin’s mega opus. Both deal with dynastic wars, and the suspicious and manipulative psychology that entails. Magic plays a relatively minor background role — this is actually historical fantasy, and far from swords-and-sorcery stuff (more on that below). The “evil” characters aren’t Sauron/Voldemort analogs, imbued with the myth of pure evil, but mere humans with the same banal incentives and passions we all share.<br /><br />It is worthwhile to recall Kay’s pedigree as an author, since that also illuminates important aspects of his work. While studying philosophy in college, he was asked to help family friends — the Tolkiens — compile and edit <i>The Silmarillion</i>. After moving to Oxford for that project, he met Dorothy Dunnett, author of epic historical fiction set in the sixteenth century.<br /><br />Kay’s trajectory as an author bridges those two influences, starting with a fairly ahistorical fantasy (<i>The Fionavar Tapestry</i> trilogy, which nevertheless draws on myths and legends), but soon, with <i>Tigana</i>, shifting to historical fantasy. <i>Tigana</i> is set in an other-world analog of medieval Italy. <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is set in an other-world analog of medieval Languedoc and Provence. His later “analogs” have moved closer to our world’s history.<br /><br />Knowing that there is some real history behind <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> might be of interest. The Languedoc/Provence portion of France was, in the middle ages, independent of Rome, and developed a distinctive culture. One important aspect was the evolution of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Court_of_love" rel="nofollow noopener">courtly love</a>, which led to notions of chivalry that are now deeply embedded in western culture, history and mythology, as well as the troubadours that propagated and memorialized this. This is transferred into <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> without significant modification.<br /><br />The other important development in Languedoc/Provence was a divergent theology. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathar_heresy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cathar Heresy</a> isn’t replicated directly. Cathar beliefs invoked a dual god — a good God of the spiritual realm, and an evil God (Satan) who created and ruled the material world. But Kay did create an analogous heretical dualism, which is central to the action. The bellicose north worships only the masculine god Corannos, whereas Arbonne elevates the feminine Rian to be his peer. The predictable result is a misogynistic religious crusade to cleanse the south of this heresy — what in our history was the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albigensian_Crusade" rel="nofollow noopener">Albigensian Crusade</a>. Even if you don’t want to remember any of the foregoing, it is interesting to note that this is possibly the first invocation of the bumpersticker “Kill them all and let God sort them out”: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arnaud_Amalric" rel="nofollow noopener">Arnaud Amalric</a>, the papal legate and inquisitor was asked how to tell the good Catholics from the evil Cathars, and he responded <i>“Caedite eos. Novit enim Dominus qui sunt emus”</i> (“Kill them all. For the Lord knoweth them that are His.”)<br /><br />But, honestly, these parallels aren’t really brought up in the book. That Eleanor of Aquitaine was probably the role model for Signe de Barbentain might mean something to aficionados of medieval history, but not most of us. (Trying to decode the parallels of other characters, events, and locales might be a distraction or a further pleasure, of course.)<br /><br />Perhaps <i>A Song For Arbonne</i> is too lyrical and meandering for some, but that is their loss. For most of us, this is a gorgeous tale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15709168392319197088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_133550439" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133550439" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133550439?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133550439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10286718932453728503">This was my first Guy Gavriel Kay, and I was not disappointed. I would recommend this book for any fans of emotional, historical epics. There's really not too much fantasy here, besides that the story takes place in a fictional world with two moons. This story centers around Gorhaut, a God-worshipping, male dominated nation that recently went through an upheaval in leadership, and Arbonne, it's neighbor. Arbonne is a Goddess worshipping nation in which troubadours are greatly admired and women h</span>
  <span id="freeText10286718932453728503" style="display:none">This was my first Guy Gavriel Kay, and I was not disappointed. I would recommend this book for any fans of emotional, historical epics. There's really not too much fantasy here, besides that the story takes place in a fictional world with two moons. This story centers around Gorhaut, a God-worshipping, male dominated nation that recently went through an upheaval in leadership, and Arbonne, it's neighbor. Arbonne is a Goddess worshipping nation in which troubadours are greatly admired and women hold positions of power. Blaise of Gorhaut is currently serving in Arbonne, although the reasons behind this are not revealed until well into the book. We get to see Arbonne through his eyes first - the "courtly love" of the troubadours which seems to make buffoons of everyone involved, the weak men of the nation who allow their wives to run around with whoever they please, and the weak monarchy, after the count has died leaving the frail, elderly countess in charge. But is Arbonne really that weak? I think that at the heart of this story is a study of the nature of women and men - what makes us strong, and what can make us fanatical and oppressive to each other. The characters are compelling and I found myself excited to hear about almost all of them - even the villains. There's some great plot twists here at the end too! A great read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10286718932453728503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1779468255" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1779468255" />
    <a title="Anirudh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16686132-anirudh"><img alt="Anirudh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359010972p2/16686132.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1779468255?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anirudh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anirudh" href="/user/show/16686132-anirudh">Anirudh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007342051" href="/book/show/8013371-a-song-for-arbonne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16686132-anirudh?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1779468255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3280076628065120559">I write this review as an aggrieved reader. What I thought this book would be like and what it actually is are so different that I begin to wonder if I am really reading the same author who wrote Tigana.<br/><br/>Many or most of you will perhaps disagree with me or my issues with the book, and even might complain that a book should not be compared to another book and must be read for what it is, rather than what it should have been. Generally I would agree. However, seeing that GGK Sacrifices so many thin</span>
  <span id="freeText3280076628065120559" style="display:none">I write this review as an aggrieved reader. What I thought this book would be like and what it actually is are so different that I begin to wonder if I am really reading the same author who wrote Tigana.<br /><br />Many or most of you will perhaps disagree with me or my issues with the book, and even might complain that a book should not be compared to another book and must be read for what it is, rather than what it should have been. Generally I would agree. However, seeing that GGK Sacrifices so many things in his book to focus on certain aspects, aspects which set him apart from other writers of the genre it is quite fair to point out that the sacrifice in this case was a bad trade. <br /><br /><b> Plot </b> Wikipedia suggests that the book is based on the Albigensian Crusade. GGK as he is known to write his series based on an actual event from history has done this in all of his works. However unlike Lions of Al Rassan, the plot hardly touches upon the crusade itself and focuses on the lives of few characters and the general troubadour culture. The death of a person is the basis of the whole story and later on we realise that there is no story. Nothing really ever happens in this book. Of course characters muse and pretend to play an intriguing political game, but it is hardly intriguing and the characters them selves are poorly contrived. The passionate loss of a country in Tigana is reduced to an affair that is barely explained among other things. One would feel no pity for anyone here except perhaps for himself for grudgingly suffering through.<br /><br /><b> Characters </b> As any GGK fan would know, his novels focus on characters and their emotions and they are what drives the story. In this case, there is not a single character I grew to like nor was there any emotion that the sacrifice of plot, action, humour and fast pace should have bought. <br /><br />The characters if one sits down and begins to dwell upon are shades of the characters in Tigana. They seem to serve the same purpose the characters in Tigana served, albeit poorly. <br />Bertran is perhaps the most infuriating character of them all. A famous poet as well as master of war. This was something I criticized in Kvothe as well. He would have been a much better character if he was any one of the two but not both. For someone who claims to have loved so dearly that he cannot forget anything about the woman dead twenty years long, he certainly seems to be enamored by many. His love so passionate that he held a grudge twenty three years despite doing so undermined his own country (Which he later claims to love more than anything) A stark contract from Robert Baratheon, who tore the world asunder for the woman he loved, only to admit later than he has forgotten her face. <br /><br />Blaise is another problem. He seems to be a very confused man for someone who has seen so much of the world. Some of his scenes and dialogues are cringeworthy at best. He has absolutely no reason to do so many things that he ends up doing that one wonders if he is reading the Wheel of Time. Emotional and Submissive men have often been the staple of GGK's books. That is not entirely true in this situation, however Blaise more than makes up for it. <br /><br />No other character is particularly noteworthy. There is a strong old female countess, a faithful and worrisome Steward. A handsome master of all duke and a female joglar who does... nothing. <br /><br />Women have been depicted as mysterious creatures fiercely beautiful forever surrounded by wide eyed meek men who wish nothing but to submit to them. This is as tiresome as the harpys Robert Jordan depicted them as. (Mind you, this is after claiming women are coinage, to be traded for political gains)<br /><br />Lastly, the villains in GGK's books are often shades of grey. Driven by their own virtues or needs and not spawns of evil. He seems to have traded those for a text book villain who is a villain for the sake of being so. <br /><br />Magic is not so prominent so as to require a separate paragraph. And looking at the author, the pace is self explanatory. Overall, a very surprising book for it carried no surprises, no charm and certainly no emotions. There are no redeeming features in this book. The only thing I enjoyed was the short reference to the triumphal arch. But a glass of water does not an oasis make. <br /><br /><b> A sad note from an otherwise lovely lute. </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3280076628065120559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
